NOTICE OF THE CHINESE CALENDAB,
Among the customs in which Uie Chinese diifer from the nations of
Europe, partkuiarly noticeable is their system of Calendar. Both Chinese
and Europeans ground their computation of time on the revolution of the
earth round the sun; but the latter reckon the civil year simply according
to that period, dividing it into twelve parts or months which have no relation
with the lunations, and the year, by the intercalation of one day at (hier-
mined intervals, is made to agree with the course of the sun ; the former,
on the contrary, make the month equal to the time of apparent revolution
of the moon round the earth and compose tbe year of twelve or thirteen
months to keep up with the sun. Their months being simply lunations，
the year does not agree with the revolution of the sun, since twelve luna-
tions do not make ii|> a solar year, being 100 short by about eleven days.
These differences make it somewhat difficult to translate dates from one
system to the other. The object of tbis paper is to give an account of the
Chinese Calendar and to supply the means of converting Chinese into
European computations and vice versa. May this modest contribution to
Chinese litterature he of some use to the student, and still better of assis-
tance to our Missionaries and our native Christians.
N. B. The Chinese Characters are romanized according (o
Wade’s system. -


OF THE DOUBLE CALENDAR.
1. The Calendars for the use of Hie people, published by Imperial
con 丨丨 rmnd，are of two kinds. The first, 奶萬年書，认 e
Calendar of ten thousand years, is an abridgement of the Calendar com-
prising 397 years, viz. from 1 624 to 2020. It gives for each year Hie
following indications: 1° the cyclical sign of the year; 2° [belong months
(of 30 days), the short months (of 29 days) and the intercalary month;
3° the cyclical sign of the Ist, 11th and 21st days of each month; 4° Hie
day and hour (referred to the meridian of Peking) at which each of
the twenty-tour solar terms begins (1).

reign, that is to the year 1624.

OF THE PUBLICATION OF THE CALENDAR.
8. Every year, on Ihe Ist of the 2nd momh, the Board of Mathematics
presents to the Emperor three copies of the An?iual Calendar for ihe
fttllowhig year, namely in Chinese, in Manchou and in Mongolian. Appro-
bation being given, it is engraved and printed. Then on the Ist of the 4th
month, two printed copies in Chinese are sent to the Fa 7i l ai' M 8
(Tmmim’J of each province，that of C/ri/， 2 - 泌直隸 excepted; Qne of
which, stamped with the seal of the Board of Mathematics, is lo be pro
served in the archives of the while the other is used for engra-
ving and printing for public use in the province.
(1) G 4 - 加曆學疑問補 ViJ. 2

9. On the Ist day of the 10 th month, early in the morning*, the Board
of Mathematics goes to offer Calendars to the Imperial court. The copies
destined to the Emperor and Empresses are borne upon a sedan-like stand
painted with figures of dragons hn 沪龍亭上 those for the Princes，
the Ministers and officers of the court being carried on eight similar stands
decorated with silk ornaments (f/VaPKn 沪練亭 They are accom-
panied by the officers of the Board with numerous attendants and the Imperial
band of music. On arriving at the first entrance of the palace, the Calendars
for £he Emperor are placed upon an ornamented stand, those for other
persons being put upon two other stands on each side. The copies for the
Emperor and his family are not stamped with the seal of the Board of
Mathematics, while the others are. The middle stand is taken into the
palace, where the officers of the Board make three genuflections, each
followed by ) 丨) rostrations，after which the Calendars are handed to
the eunuchs who present tliem to the Emperor, the Empress-mother, the
Empress and other [)ersons of the seraglio, two copies being given to each,
viz. one in Chinese and one in Manchou. The master of cermonies then
proceeds to ihe entrance of the palace where【he two otlier stands were
left, and where the Princes, the Ministers with the civil and military
mandarins, both Manchous and Mongols all in robes of state are in attend-
ance. The master of ceremonies reads the Imperial decree of publication
of the Calendars, namely: 'The Emperor presents you all with ihe Annual
Calendar cf the year, and promulgates it throughout the Empire n , which
proclamation is heard kneeling-. Then follow three genuflections and nine
prostrations, after which all receive the Calendar on their knees, the
Princes two copies, one in Chinese and one in Manchou, the ministers and
other officers only one, each in his own language. Lastly the Corean envoy,
who must attend every year on that day, is presented kneeling with one
hundred Chinese copies, to take home with him.
10. In Uie provinces，tlie 臺 藩臺 （TVeaswra’J，after getting
some printed copies of tlie Calendar stamped with a special seal, also on
the Ist of the 10th month, sends them on a sedan-like stand to the Viceroy
or Governor, accompanied by the mandarin 纽 纽 ，-八即i理問
廳， who is intrusted wilh 旦 卫旦 〇i〇fUie Calendar. The Viceroy oi，
Governor receives them to the sound of music anJ of three cannon shots.
The Calendars being set upon a stand between two tapers in the tribunal,
the Viceroy or Governor, in robes of state, approaches the stand, and


turning towards that quarter where Peking is situated, makes three genu-
flections and nine prostrations, after which cermony he reverently receives
the Calendars. The Treasurer sends the Calendar to ail the civil and
military Mandarins, all of whom, except those of inferior degree, receive it
with the same forms. Any copies left are sold to the people. The reprint-
ing of the Calendar is forbidden under a penalty (except in Fa 2 ，e/^>n 建福
建and分n分 w? 砂 1 廣東 where il is tolerated). If therefore any copy
is found without seal or with a false one, its author is sought after and
punished. Falsification of the Calendar is punished with death; whoever
reprints the Annual Calendar is liable lo 100 blows and two months
cangue.

15. A METHOD TO FIND THE SIGN OF THE SEXAGENARY CYCXjE
CORRESPONDING TO ANY YEAR BEFORE OR AFTER CHRIST.
The first year before the Giiristian era corresponds to the 57th
cyclical sign ， 庚申， and the first year of the Ghrislian era
to the 58tti 酉 辛酉 .Hence :
I°. If required to find the sign of a year before the Christian era, add
2 to the figure of the year. If tlie sum is inferior ， 60， subtract it from
60: the difference gives the cyclical sign of the year. If the sum exceeds
59, divide it by 60 and subtract the remainder from 60: the difference
gives the cyclical sign. Let it he for instance 43 B.G. 43 4- 2 45. 60—
45 = 15, the cyclical sign for the said year is then the 15th, that is
寅 戊寅 .一 Again 202 B.C. 202 + 2 二 2(M. 204 = 3 x 60 + 24.
60 —24 = 36; the cyclical sign therefore is the 36th, or
己亥.
2°. If the question is of a year of the Christian era, then subtract 3
from the number of the year. If the difference is smaller than 60, it gives
ihe cyclical sign; if it is greater than 59 divide it by 60, and the remainder
will give 'the cyclical sign. ]f there is no remainder, the cyclical sign is the
GOlh. Thus for A.D. 62, subtract 3, the difference is 59, the cyclical sign is
then the 地 地 . 壬戎. 一Again A.D. 221, subtract 3, the diffe-
rence is 218, whidi divided by 60 leaves a remainder of 38: the cyclical sign
is iherefore the 38th . 辛丑. 一Ag'ain A. D. 243. 243—3
=：240, which number divided by 60 leaves no remainder; the cyclical
sign therefore is Uie 60lh . 癸亥. 一lf we take the 2nd year
after Christ let us first add 60, then from the sum 62 subtract 3 : Hie
diiFerence 59 gives the number of the cyclical sign, Jen x -hsu 2^:

a birth, and consisting of the four cyclical signs of the year, month,
day and hour at which he was born. Let us for instance suppose one to
have been born at 8 p.i7j. on the 9th day of the 12th moon of the 9lii

OF THE YEAR.
i 7, U was 7，a 4 -nao ， 太撓， Minister lo Uie Emperor ‘//wcm// 2-“，黃
帝， who，by command of his Sovereign, devised (he sexagenary cycle.
‘//wany 2 -" b 黃帝 began lo reign 2697 B.d and tlu‘ Gist year of.his reign
was taken for the first cyclical sign. The interval from the first year of
M fi? totheyear 1624 comprises exactly 72 cycles. This year
1624, commencing the 73rd cycle, is also designated by the first sign and
the subsequent years follow in order. Since the sign of that year, wliich
was bissextile, is forined with the Ist _6ranc/i !Tsu ， 子， it follows that all
the years the signs of which are formed with the Ist, slh and 9th Bran-
c加s hu 6 子 67^en 辰 辰 a申 从如 1 申 are bissextile, with the exception
of the last years of centuries the numbers of which are not multiples of 4.
Thus the years 1700, 1800； 1900, 2100 are not bissextile, though their
cyclical signs are formed with the said Branches.
18. As the twelve Branches which go to form the cyclical signs are
designated by names of animals, the years also are indicated by the same.
It is usual with parents to inform their children of the date of their binh,
not by saying in what year of the cjcle or of the Emperors reign they
were born, bul by telling them that they belong to such or such an animal,
which peculiarity is easier to remember (1).
19. If we give credit to Hie learned, that custom of designating the
successive years by the names of twelve animals originated among the
northern tribes and was introduced into China about A.D. 48, when after
the conclusion of the treaty of peace between the Emperor Wu^ti^
光武帝， of the ‘ftm d 逢 dynasly, with //'u K 呼乾邪 King
(l) The Chinese reckon the years of one J s life, not from the day but froiu
the year of his birth. For instance if a child was born on the last day of the 12(h
montb, on the following day 5 which is the Ist of the Ist nionlh of the next year,
it is said to be Liang i 'sui lk Wt, two years, although there may not have
elapsed a whole day since its birth. Properly il is to say, not that it is two
years old, but that It has lived within two years. This way t)f reckoning is
explained at the end of the Annual Calendar.

20. There have been endless discussions 3boul ibis custom, some
pretending lo see deep symbolism in the nature and sSupe of the different
animals and giving reasons why they should severally be attached to the
twelve Branches ; but opinions on that subject are widely discrepant and
the more propable sentimont seems to be that the choice of the various
animals was altogether arbitrary.
0? THE LUNAR MONTH.
21. At the end of 19 years the sun, the earth and the moon are found
in the same relative positions as at the begining of Uiat period, and Ihe
new moou begins almost at the same moment as before Tliis interval ot
19 years comprises 235 lunar months, generally 125 long months of 30
days, WO short months of 29 days (but sometimes 124 longsimi 111 short
months), and 7 intercalarfj months. The year of twelve months is called
a common year; that of thirteen months, an intercalary year.
22. The time ot a lunation varies from less than 29 days 8 hoars to
more than 29 days 19 hours, and there can be three consecutive long
months. The first day is that within the interval of which, from midnight
to miflnight, the moment of the new moon falls: the month is longxi from
the day of its new moon to Ihe following new moon there are 30 civil
days; shorty if there are only 29 days. Should for instance the moment
of the new moon take place at 1 Hi. 59m. p.m. ; that day will be made the
Ist of the month, though it contains but Im. of the new lunation.
23. All the months, except the intercalary month, are designated
by the several branches: that whose new moon immediately precedes the
winter solstice, assumes the branch -f and all the others in succes-
sion take the other as is seen in the following table.

OF THE INTERCALARY MONTH.
31. The system of adding a montii to the year was established by the
Emperor ； 叩 2 ，以黃帝； jbout 2637 B.G. (67/iA 3 -c/;i V 史記 V01. 26).
The period of 19 years comprises 7 intercalary years, each of 13 months.
The intercalary inontli is called /wn 4 -y 以从 ， 閏月， that is lo say a monUi
made up of remnants. The 7 intercalary years in the period oM 9 are: the
3rd, 6th, 9lh, Mth, |4th, 17th and 19th.
32. There have been several sjstems in use for making the
intercalation.
I°. To repeat the last month of the year.
2°. To insert an intercalary month ;ifter each interval of 32 or 33
months in such order that on the 3rd year the 9th month should be
duplicated, on t. e 6th the 6th, on the 9th the 3rd, on the 11th the 11th,
on the 14th the Bth, on tiie 17th the 4th, on the 19th tbe I2lh.
3°. The intercalation depends from the number of days remaining
after the winter solstice to the end of the month on the year before. Thus
for instance, if on the year before the intercalary, the winter solstice occurs


on the 24th of the 11th month, the intercalary month in the subsequent
year will be after the s(h or 6th month according as the said I llh month
was a short or a long one, Jf the solstice occurred before the full moon,
the days in excess would be counted to the full moon, and if their number
exceeded 12, the difference with 12 would have to be taken.
4°. In the 4th system the following conditions must be fulfilled：
1) that the winter solstice always fall in the Hth month, the summer sol-
stice in the sth, the vernal equinox in the 2nd, and the autumnal equinox
in the Bth; 2) that the duplicated month be such that the sun do not pass
from one zodiacal sign to another within ils interval ； hence in the inter-
calaryinonth there can occur but i節氣，狀
in which the sun enters the 15th degree of a zodiacal sign, and no
chH 4* principal term, in which the sun enters the Ist degree of a
zodiacal sign (1); lienee also the month preceding the intercalary has a
principal term on some one of its last days, and the subsequent month has
ai principal term about its new moon; 3) that the 11th, 12th and Ist
months never be duplicated, because the solar terms which precede and
follow the winter solstice are shorter th;tn the others. This 4th system
is the one now followed by the Board of Mathematics.
33. The series of 19 years, in the interval of which there occur 7
intercalary years, is identical with the cycle of the Golden number, tlie
Isi year of (he former corresponding to the 10th of the latter, the 2nd to
the 11th. . the 19th to the 9th: hence all the years having for Golden
number 12, 15, 18, 1, 7 or 9, are intercalary (2). If however
(i) See below N n 36.
('2) We herewith give a method of finding the Golden number for any year,
before or after Christ. The fir&t year before the Christian era is under N° I,
and the Ist of the Christian era undrr N° 2. Hence : I°. to find tbe Golden
知尸 for a year before the Ghristian era, stibli'act 2 from its mimber, divide
by 19: the remainder subtracted from 19 will be the number. Thus for 2634
B.C. 2634 2 = 2632, which divided by 19 leaven 16 as a remainder； 19
—lO =9, which is the Golden ， ，
2°. To nnd the Golden number of a year, of the Christian era, divide by 19,
and add 1 to the remainder: the sum is tl e Golden number. Thus for A.D.
1884. Divide by 19 and to tbe remainder add 1, the sum 4 Is the Golden
number for the year 1884*


it happens that the addition of one month would make the winter solstice
fall before the 11th month, the intercalation is deferred till after the 2nd
month of the following year; for the rule is that the winter solstice must
fall in the llih month, and also that (he lllh, 12tli and Ist months never
be reduplicated. This explains how the years 1794, 1813, 1889, 1908,
1927, 1946, 1965, 2003, whose Golden number is 9, have only 12
months, the intercalation being put off lo the next years under (he Golden
number 10, but the third years after those, under the Golden number
12, are intercalary as usual.

thanks to the carelessness of the officers of the Board of Mathematics, the

the Blh month ; liial year in fact, being under Ihe Golden number
9, was to he intercalary, and that Bili montli ；ilso fulfilled (he condition
of containing bat a l5 sectional term : yel from the
intercaiation thus made it resulted ihat the winter solstice fell in the 10th
month and the vernal equinox of Uie following year in the Ist month.
Tljc Emperor CV/iahc/dn,? ， 嘉慶， informed of ’lhe circumstances, gave
orders that the error should be corrected and a month be added, not after
Hie Bth of that year, but after the 2nd of the next which also contained
bat a IP by which correction the winter solstice was
made to fall in the l.lih monUi arul the vernal equinox in the 2nd.

VIII.
OF THE DAY.
39. A common civil year consists of 354 or 355 days, while the
intercalary year has not less than 383 or more than 385 days. Different
from those is the legal year, to determine the duration of banishment, or
to fix a term past which a penalty may be incurred for non-fulfilment of a
law; it is taken equal to 360 days, so that in common years the deficiency
from short months must bo supplied, and in intercalary years the days
in excess must be taken off.
40. The time of mourning is fixed at 3,5, 9, 12 or 27 months
according to the degree of consangunity or ailinily. It is computed from
the day of decease or from that on which notice of the death has been
received. The days in default from short months are not supplied, but if
there occurs an intercalary month, it is not counted eittier.
41. The hour at which the day is reckoned to begin has varied in
different times : under 办 夏办 nasty (2205 8.G.), it was taken at
Uie beginning of dawn or 4 a. m. ; at tiie begining of the Man" d商
dynasty (1766 B.G. ) at noon, and afterwards at cock-crow or 2 a* ni.;
under the d 周 dynasty (1122 8.C.) and up to our days, at midnight.
42. Each day is designated hy a cyclical sign. If computation is
made from the time of ‘"wan 分 w -" 4 黃帝 when this custom originakni，
the Ist day of the Ist month of the year 1624 of the Christian era is found
to be under Ihe ， 丙辰， and the series follows down in
order. To facilitate the search for the sign of any day, we give in this
Concordance of the Calendars the sign of the Ist day of every month.

A CONCORDANCE
OF THE
CHINESE AND EUROPEAN CALENDARS.
■•jrOJOo—
ADVERTISEMENT.
I°, This small work comprises 377 years, viz. from 1644 to 2020,
according to the # or Calendar of ten thou-
sand years, published by Imperial command.
2°. There is a column for every year, which is subdivided into five
vertical lines.
3°. The figure on top of a column indicates the year of the Christian
era; the letter B. means bissextile.
4° Under this figure there is the name of the Emperor of the reigning
dynasty.
s°. The cyclical characters under the figure denoting the year of the
Emperors reign designate that same year.
6°. The first vertical line of each column contains the Cycles of the
12 lunar months.
7°. The figures in the second vertical line indicate the month of the
year, a capital letter standing for a long month of 30 days, a small one for
a short month of days, and an asterisk for an intercalary month.
B°. The third vertical line gives the corresponding： European months.
9°. The figures in the fourth line are the dajs of European months
corresponding to the first day of the lunar months.
fiftii line gives the cyclical characters for the first day of
the lunar month.
11°. The figure, with letter D, before it, at the end of the second line,
marks the day of the last lunar month which corresponds to the Ist of
January (1), and the cyclical characters on the right hand side belong to
that same day.
12°. The figure at the end of each column gives the number of days
in that year.
(1) Russia, refusing to adopt the Gregorian re-form of the calendar, did not take off
ten days in October 1582, and counts the years 1700, 1800, 1900 as bifsixlile. They now
(I90i) begin the year on the day which, for other European nations, is January 14th.
